<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5386364917911956367">Something new has come up between the Discworld's ancient rival cites of Ankh-Morpork and Al-Khali. Literally<br /><br />It's up island, rising out of Discworld's sea, uninhabited and claimed by both cities.<br /><br />Under International Law this situation clearly falls under the ancient doctrine of <em>Acquiris Quodcumque Rapis</em> ("You Get What You Grab"). And everyone wants to grab. Besides, the Al-</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5386364917911956367">Something new has come up between the Discworld's ancient rival cites of Ankh-Morpork and Al-Khali. Literally<br /><br />It's up island, rising out of Discworld's sea, uninhabited and claimed by both cities.<br /><br />Under International Law this situation clearly falls under the ancient doctrine of <em>Acquiris Quodcumque Rapis</em> ("You Get What You Grab"). And everyone wants to grab. Besides, the Al-Khalians may have invented algebra, astronomy and alcohol, but hey don't have a word for lawyer, and how can you talk to people like that?<br /><br />Since there's no basis for negotiation, it's down to the long-suffering Commander Vimes of the City Watch to deal with a crime as awful that there's no law against it.<br /><br />It's called war.<br /><br />Ankh-Morpork has been at peace for a century, and so has Al-Khali. But now there are people on both sides who think it's time to give was a chance, and will happily help it on its way with a few murders...<br /><br />Modern war needs modern weapons. Unfortunately, Ankh-Morpork got rich making and selling them to Al-Khali. But it's just possible that salvation lies in the hands of the great inventive genius Leonard of Quirm, whose sketchbooks are filled with devices for killing people, flying through the air, and weighing cheese.<br /><br />Maybe it's in his boat tat travels under water--Leonard calls it a "Going Under-The-Water-Safely Device", or "metal sinking fish thing" for short. (Just because he's an inventor doesn't mean he's good at naming stuff.) But this is carrying something else--a device that so powerful that it can finish any war.<br /><br />But don't be alarmed. It's fantasy. It all happens on Discworld, where greed and ignorance influence human behavior, politicians pursue was for selfish ends, and perfectly ordinary people occasionally act like raving idiots.<br /><br />A world, in short, totally unlike our own.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5386364917911956367">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_444475885">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/444475885" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5238423-nathan" class="left imgcol" title="Nathan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1352775557p2/5238423.jpg" alt="Nathan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/444475885?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 10, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5238423-nathan" class="user" title="Nathan">Nathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/47990.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552154161" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=author-male" class="actionLinkLite">author-male</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=high-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">high-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=rated-4-star" class="actionLinkLite">rated-4-star</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=read-2012" class="actionLinkLite">read-2012</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=read-before-goodreads" class="actionLinkLite">read-before-goodreads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=read-2014" class="actionLinkLite">read-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer444475885">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8505235734483605227"><a rel="nofollow" href="http://fantasyreviewbarn.com/the-complete-discworld-re-read/">The Complete Discworld Reread</a><br /><br />I am a Granny Weatherwax guy. With no hesitation. You ask me which Pratchett story arc is the best and I will yell to the world how awesome the witches of Lancre are. But I got a dirty secret; it wasn’t always this way to me. Granny is the other woman, the one I left my first love for. Before I fell in love with Granny, I was a city watch man through and through. Reading Jingo again has reminded me exactly why.<br /><br />It is not the best book of the Discworld series, not by a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8505235734483605227"><a rel="nofollow" href="http://fantasyreviewbarn.com/the-complete-discworld-re-read/">The Complete Discworld Reread</a><br /><br />I am a Granny Weatherwax guy. With no hesitation. You ask me which Pratchett story arc is the best and I will yell to the world how awesome the witches of Lancre are. But I got a dirty secret; it wasn’t always this way to me. Granny is the other woman, the one I left my first love for. Before I fell in love with Granny, I was a city watch man through and through. Reading Jingo again has reminded me exactly why.<br /><br />It is not the best book of the Discworld series, not by a long shot. Even against the other city watch books it probably sits in, well, bottom half for sure. Behind Guards! Guards!, Feet of Clay, and Night Watch definitely. Been too long since I read The Fifth Elephant, but I remember it being pretty good too. So, let’s give it the fifth spot and go from there. So the fourth or fifth best book in my second favorite subplot of a large series and I still flew through and gobbled up every damn word.<br /><br />A small, tactically worthless island appears out of nowhere between Ankh-Morpork and Klatch. Of course both sides claim it and suddenly peaceful relations turn sour. A diplomatic mission from Klatch goes sour and soon enough war appears to be the only result. But it should be over quickly right, just as soon as those foreign dogs get a taste of cold steel they will run to the hills, right?<br /><br />Not the most subtle of book, but Pratchett is known to hide a single piccolo in a brass band. If you are looking for the in jokes they are always there, but there are plenty of big jokes to play around with even if you don’t see them. This time we are dealing with national politics, war, racism and immigration, and of course, national jingoism.<br /><br />There is an early scene in this book that sums up everything I love about Pratchett, and it is only a few pages long. But our good friends Colon and Nobby are watching the cities jingoistic sailors prepare for way, specifically a sign painter who has missed a letter ‘e’ in the Pride of Ankh-Morpork. And while they wait for their minor schadenfreude Colon ‘educates’ Nobby on the politics of the situation; strait from the reliable sources of ‘what some bloke told me in a pub.’ Nobby sits and pokes holes in every amount of Colon’s non PC argument by agreeing with in in a very telling way. It is a conversation that could be held in any bar across the country and it is damn near perfect. All tied together at the end by the two sharing a minor victory as the man painting his ship finds the mistake they have been watching for.<br /><br />At this point the City Watch as grown so large that only a few members get any actual development in the book, most just stick to their old roles or give a bit of comic relief. Carrot is established at this point; certainly not a king but a man people can’t help but follow (all the way to a foreign country if need be). Sadly Angua may as well be invisible; this is a rare Pratchett outing that forgets to give the female characters anything to do at all. Nobbs learns a bit more about love, and Vimes learns once again that he is bought and sold. I had never before noticed that Vimes shows apparent growth in each book of his story arc, yet in each is in the exact same place by the end; he just gets a new title or concession thrown his way after the Patrician is done pulling all the right strings (well, wasn’t the Patrician this time, but strings were still pulled).<br /><br />Not sure why I don’t rate this one as high as some of the earlier ones. Perhaps it is because all the watch is present but few have much to do. The relative lack of Angua especially is missed. It is very over the top in making its point this time around; the Ankh-Morporkians are so stupidly sure of themselves against their ‘uncivilized’ neighbors it should be laughable (if history didn’t show people with that misplaced confidence time and again). And of course the whole Vimes is in a rut thing is starting to show.<br /><br />But I liked it better than I remembered. Humor? Still top notch. If you don’t find a zombie catching rats and calling it a foreign disease hilarious you have no soul (especially when you learn of the cure). And who can forget rival street gangs putting their thumbs in their ears and chanting together a silly little nonsense. Good stuff. And I have always wondered where you find lowerglyphics, what it means when seaweed is wet, and why someone would carry around a life sized inflatable donkey. I enjoyed Vimes’ new rival, 71 Hour Ahmed, who has a very unique way of keeping people off guard. I was also fairly impressed about the eventual conclusion to the early stories crime (the war itself came later). Everyone suspects everyone, even themselves, and both sides wanted to claim the criminal as their own is a different type of thing.<br /><br />Not the best, but a solid entry.<br /><br />4 Stars</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8505235734483605227">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1296573313">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1296573313" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8386404-richard" class="left imgcol" title="Richard"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1351707681p2/8386404.jpg" alt="Richard" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1296573313?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 17, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8386404-richard" class="user" title="Richard">Richard</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/833436.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552145985" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/8386404-richard?shelf=comedy" class="actionLinkLite">comedy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/8386404-richard?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1296573313">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer483808751706335798">6/10<br /><br />This is the weakest Watch novel I’ve read to date, quite simply the bar has been set too high in the previous novels meaning that a small blip is quite a noticeable drop in quality. Whilst entertaining in parts it became a bit of a chore to finish this one with a very convoluted plot which started tangling itself up the further things progressed and I was losing the threads the longer I read. <br /><br />The first 1/3 of the novel is the usual top notch quality writing with a mystery being looked into b</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText483808751706335798">6/10<br /><br />This is the weakest Watch novel I’ve read to date, quite simply the bar has been set too high in the previous novels meaning that a small blip is quite a noticeable drop in quality. Whilst entertaining in parts it became a bit of a chore to finish this one with a very convoluted plot which started tangling itself up the further things progressed and I was losing the threads the longer I read. <br /><br />The first 1/3 of the novel is the usual top notch quality writing with a mystery being looked into by the Watch, witty back and forths between Vimes and Vetinari and the usual capers that happen throughout a normal Watch novel set in Ankh-Morpork with all the side characters. Then things take a sharp turn and go way off left-field and the Watch go invading like an army in a foreign country, Vetinari enlists Nobbs and Colon to do some spying whilst travelling in a submarine and the humour and high standards that are normally expected take a sharp drop. <br /><br />I’ve said it previously about the Watch novels, the best parts are the interactions between Vimes and Vetinari. These are few and far between in this novel and swapping Vimes for Nobbs on Colon definitely didn’t have the same impact for me. Other enjoyable parts are Vimes struggling to work out how a certain murder has happened whereas this took a back seat in this story and at times I’d forgot that a murder had taken place. <br /><br />I’d been warned that this one wasn’t viewed as highly as the others before reading it so that might have had an impact on me when things went a little boring but fingers crossed for a return to form in the next novel and back to the usual hijinks of the previous books.<br /><br />If you like this try: “The Island of the Sequined Love Nun” by Christopher Moore</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="483808751706335798">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_763300567">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/763300567" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/13954899-kathleen" class="left imgcol" title="Kathleen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1365166675p2/13954899.jpg" alt="Kathleen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/763300567?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 13, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13954899-kathleen" class="user" title="Kathleen">Kathleen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/47990.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552154161" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=fantasy-scifi" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-scifi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=discworld" class="actionLinkLite">discworld</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer763300567">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15104412141730554207">I love this Discworld "City Watch" sub-series. Love the characters: Commander Sam Vimes, Lord Vetinari, Captain Carrot, the troll Detritus, and the whole multi-species gang of street patrollers. <br /><br /><strong>About the title, from lspace Annotated Pratchett:</strong> "By jingo!" is an archaic, jocular oath, of obscure origin, used in Britain in the 18th and 19th centuries. The word -- with derived forms such as 'jingoism' and 'jingoistic' -- became associated with aggressive, militaristic nationalism as a result of a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15104412141730554207">I love this Discworld "City Watch" sub-series. Love the characters: Commander Sam Vimes, Lord Vetinari, Captain Carrot, the troll Detritus, and the whole multi-species gang of street patrollers. <br /><br /><strong>About the title, from lspace Annotated Pratchett:</strong> "By jingo!" is an archaic, jocular oath, of obscure origin, used in Britain in the 18th and 19th centuries. The word -- with derived forms such as 'jingoism' and 'jingoistic' -- became associated with aggressive, militaristic nationalism as a result of a popular song dating from the Turko-Russian war of 1877-78, which began:<blockquote>
  <em>We don't want to have to fight, <br />but by Jingo if we do <br />We've got the ships, we've got the men, <br />we've got the money too. </em>
</blockquote><br /> The plot and satire is focused on empire-building and patriotism, with martial law in Ankh-Morpork and Morporkians warring on Klatchian sands. Parody comes complete with cultural stereotype (boiled eyeballs, anyone?), xenophobia, camels, deserts, and feuding desert tribes. Where's the oil?<br /><br />This cartoon by Tom Gauld sums it up!<br /><img width="400" height="220" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1437859582i/15637278._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Funny dialogue, especially between Colon and Nobbs: Page 29: "The Klatchians invented nothing...They came up with zero.'" ( -- and that's even worse than nothing!)<br /><br />I loved the scene that unfolded when Lord Vetinari sent the stymied Sam Vimes a letter with only his title on it: Sir Samuel Vimes, Knight. Really enjoyed the hysterical dialogue between Nobby and Colon, while Vetinari an unwilling audience. And the storm at sea, with Vimes refusing to shorten sail or batten down. Poor Jenkins! But I never understood what (whose magic) caused that storm. <br /><br />This book is also about football (foot-the-ball) and submarines (Going-Under-the-Water-Safely Device). It's about gender, stubborn mules, and flying carpets. Old school, old rule, and the oldest of murders — fratricide. <br /><br /><br /><strong>Quibbles: </strong> Sometimes the inner commentary bogs down the pace. Some puns have me rolling my eyes. Also, Pratchett makes too many jokes about fat people. Willikins the butler joins the militia in this book (his immature gullible behavior seemed quite out of character in the sequels, especially In <a title="Thud! (Discworld, #34; City Watch #7) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/62530.Thud___Discworld___34__City_Watch__7_">Thud!</a> and <a title="Snuff (Discworld, #39; City Watch #8) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8785374.Snuff__Discworld___39__City_Watch__8_">Snuff</a>).<br /><br />Also see "Jingo" illustration, created by Marc Simonetti, here: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://kemar.blogs.3dvf.com/2010/11/02/va-ten-guerre-jingo-by-sir-terry-pratchett/" target="_blank">http://kemar.blogs.3dvf.com/2010/11/0...</a><br /><br /><strong> Series:</strong> Here are the Discworld books about the City Watch and its commander Sam Vimes, in reading order:<br /><br />Guards! Guards! (1989) <br />Theatre of Cruelty (free online, only 4 pages)<br />Men at Arms (1993)<br />Feet of Clay (1996)<br />Jingo (1997) <br />The Fifth Elephant (1999) <br />Night Watch (2002) <br />Thud (2005) <br />Snuff (2011) <br />(Series links at <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/106221-discworld---ankh-morpork-city-watch" target="_blank">https://www.goodreads.com/series/1062...</a>)<br /><br />Oh--and Unseen Academicals (2009) is not really City Watch, but a few characters overlap.<br /><br /><strong>Related "Modernization" Series: </strong> Also, there are some spin-off books that cross-over with the "City Watch" sub-series. These books introduce new-fangled notions to the twin cities of Ankh-Morpork. The books feature <strong>con-man turned hero, Moist Von Lipvig, </strong> and a few characters from the City Watch. <a title="Going Postal (Discworld, #33; Moist von Lipwig, #1) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64222.Going_Postal__Discworld___33__Moist_von_Lipwig___1_">Going Postal</a> describes the invention and proliferation of postage stamps (loved it) and <a title="Making Money (Discworld, #36) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116296.Making_Money__Discworld___36_">Making Money</a>, depicts the creation of paper money to replace gold (also very good). Then there is <a title="Raising Steam (Discworld, #40) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11275323.Raising_Steam__Discworld___40_">Raising Steam</a>, where Lord Vetinari makes Moist Von Lipvig introduce the steam train to the City, making it popular and profitable. (Pratchett's last book in the series, and a little weak.)<br /><br />Also, <a title="The Truth (Discworld, #25) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567670.The_Truth__Discworld___25_">The Truth</a> depicts the invention of a printing press and the subsequent introduction of widespread newspapers into society. Satire deals with the wholesale acceptance of lies when ink-validated. William de Worde (he-he) is the city's first investigative journalist, sleuthing out possible murder and a plot to overthrow Lord Vetinari. This book does not feature Moist Von Lipvig, but it has some City Watch characters. <br /><br />Finally, <a title="Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10_">Moving Pictures</a> introduces the film industry to the eager citizens of Ankh-Morpork. This book does not include Moist Von Lipvig. I didn't care as much for it, but it's brimming with satire and some scenes are great.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15104412141730554207">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1231529237525657958">Con: largely disposable within the context of Discworld and feels a little like an afterthought. Highly patronising, with vague suggestions of some somewhat iffy political assumptions. Inadequate plot (partly I think on purpose).<br /><br /><br />Pro: very funny. Surprisingly enjoyable. A chance to see the Watch characters in a slightly more normal situation and at a relaxed pace, at least in the first half of the book.<br /><br /><br />Summary: I always remembered the downsides and thought this was one of the worst Discworld nove</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1231529237525657958">Con: largely disposable within the context of Discworld and feels a little like an afterthought. Highly patronising, with vague suggestions of some somewhat iffy political assumptions. Inadequate plot (partly I think on purpose).<br /><br /><br />Pro: very funny. Surprisingly enjoyable. A chance to see the Watch characters in a slightly more normal situation and at a relaxed pace, at least in the first half of the book.<br /><br /><br />Summary: I always remembered the downsides and thought this was one of the worst Discworld novels; it's definitely in the bottom half or bottom third, but it's actually much more enjoyable that I thought it was. Shouldn't be on many favourites lists, but you needn't actively avoid it when reading the Watch books.<br /><br /><br />My review is <a rel="nofollow" href="http://vacuouswastrel.wordpress.com/2014/11/20/jingo-by-terry-pratchett/">over on my blog as usual.</a><br /><br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/284305048?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 09, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7232207-fiona" class="user" title="Fiona">Fiona</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/47990.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552154161" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7232207-fiona?shelf=read-in-2012" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7232207-fiona?shelf=politics-exclamation-mark" class="actionLinkLite">politics-exclamation-mark</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7232207-fiona?shelf=warning-magic-ahead" class="actionLinkLite">warning-magic-ahead</a>,

              <a href="/review/list/7232207-fiona?shelf=worldview-shapers" class="actionLinkLite">worldview-shapers</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7232207-fiona?shelf=read-in-2015" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer284305048">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10425023122180522222">I FOUND COMMANDER VIMES IN A CHARITY SHOP *happy dance* - you must know how rare this is. Yoink, HAVING THAT.<br /><br />------------<br /><br />From 2012:<br /><br />It's not very often in the next few months that I'm going to get to read a whole book in a day, so I thought, in for a penny, in for a pound, took a Sunday off and settled down with Commander Vimes and a bar of Dairy Milk.<br /><br />It was a good choice. Of course it was. I've been recommended 'Jingo' several times, and it's perfectly obvious why: it's exactly my kind of thing.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10425023122180522222">I FOUND COMMANDER VIMES IN A CHARITY SHOP *happy dance* - you must know how rare this is. Yoink, HAVING THAT.<br /><br />------------<br /><br />From 2012:<br /><br />It's not very often in the next few months that I'm going to get to read a whole book in a day, so I thought, in for a penny, in for a pound, took a Sunday off and settled down with Commander Vimes and a bar of Dairy Milk.<br /><br />It was a good choice. Of course it was. I've been recommended 'Jingo' several times, and it's perfectly obvious why: it's exactly my kind of thing. A lot of page time is spent on my favourite supporting cast (Vetinari, Carrot, Angua) and Lady Sybil gets a good few pages of being wonderful as well. Including one where she's haphazardly knitting. If I were a more sentimental sort, I'd tell you know that we'd TOTALLY be best friends. As it is, I'm too busy giggling in glee at Carrot's myriad organisation of football matches.<br /><br />There seems to be a bit of a theme going on in my reading of late: a lot of it seems to have a very strong pacifist message. Vimes is right up there with Atticus Finch in terms of really shiny upholders of the law, and in 'Jingo' the law-abiding, but also strongly pacifist, message is possibly the strongest I've ever seen it in a Pratchett book.<br /><br />Anyway, I loved it. It was a bit confusing at times - I still didn't completely get the whole business about 71 hours - hence four stars instead of five. However, I think that's a long Sunday afternoon well spent.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10425023122180522222">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14146557?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 09, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/858949-chris" class="user" title="Chris">Chris</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/833436.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552145985" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=pratchett" class="actionLinkLite">pratchett</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14146557">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15495362489901705191">A strange foreign country across the sea, a moneyed aristocracy eager to send young men off to war, political operatives using a flimsy pretext to incite nationalist and racist fervor to encourage said war....<br /><br />Sounds unpleasantly familiar, which is probably why I was drawn to reading this book.<br /><br />This is one of the Watch books in the Discworld series, and like so many of those books deals with the intersection between law and politics. In this case, a mysterious island surfaces halfway between the g</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15495362489901705191">A strange foreign country across the sea, a moneyed aristocracy eager to send young men off to war, political operatives using a flimsy pretext to incite nationalist and racist fervor to encourage said war....<br /><br />Sounds unpleasantly familiar, which is probably why I was drawn to reading this book.<br /><br />This is one of the Watch books in the Discworld series, and like so many of those books deals with the intersection between law and politics. In this case, a mysterious island surfaces halfway between the great city-state of Ankh-Morpork and the desert empire of Klatch. Naturally, both nations claim the island as their own, and tempers flare. Next thing you know, there's armies being built, speeches being made and there's war in the air. And Sir Samuel Vimes, the commander of the Watch, is certain that someone is up to something, that there's a crime being committed. It might be a crime on the scale of nations, but it is a crime nonetheless.<br /><br />It's a great book, and timely, like so many of the more recent Discworld books. Pratchett has a deft pen and a great eye for the way the world truly works. And when seen through the jaded and cynical lens that is Sam Vimes, the hypocrisy and double-dealing become all that clearer. One line popped out at me:<br /><br /> "It had all sounded straightforward in Ankh-Morpork, he thought. We were going to sail into Klatch and be in al-Khali by teatime, drinking sherbet with pliant young women in the Rhoxie. The Klatchians would take one look at our weapons and run away...."<br /><br />This was published back in 1997, by the way, well before we launched our own poorly thought-out, baseless invasion of a land and a people we didn't understand. Go Terry.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15495362489901705191">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/833436.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552145985" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2863963-andree?shelf=2014" class="actionLinkLite">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer404004529">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6492199093644409177">Enjoyed it a lot. Didn't love it.<br /><br />Thing is, I often enjoy Terry Pratchett. Particularly the books featuring either the Watch, or the witches. I like the books better when they're set in a familiar location.<br /><br />I really enjoyed the first half of this, with the watch sort of blundering around Ankh Morpok, with hilarious results. The Watch doing any kind of detective work is always fun!times. And I really enjoy Vimes and Sybil together.<br /><br />I also really enjoyed the last sixty pages or so. Vimes' butler is p</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6492199093644409177">Enjoyed it a lot. Didn't love it.<br /><br />Thing is, I often enjoy Terry Pratchett. Particularly the books featuring either the Watch, or the witches. I like the books better when they're set in a familiar location.<br /><br />I really enjoyed the first half of this, with the watch sort of blundering around Ankh Morpok, with hilarious results. The Watch doing any kind of detective work is always fun!times. And I really enjoy Vimes and Sybil together.<br /><br />I also really enjoyed the last sixty pages or so. Vimes' butler is particularly great. And Carrot has grown on me a lot.<br /><br />This book made me laugh out loud a number of times.<br /><br />But there is a lull in the middle. In the desert, outside of the familiarity of the setting, things drag. And some of the odd combinations of people didn't quite work. (For example, I like Vetinari best when he's juxtaposed with Vimes, not Nobby.) Also, there's a definite plot whole related to Anguna.<br /><br />The other thing is, I suspect I won't rememmber more than a few details in a week. Maybe three. Because TP's books don't stay with me. I enjoy them while I read them, but they're often ephemeral. They're clever, extremely clever, and extremely entertaining, but sometimes they lack something in the core to centre around. There's a lack of character development, or something.<br /><br />(This is not always the case; when TP is at his best, the characters do drive the story, as opposed to the clever intellectual exercise cunningly dressed up in nonsense. Not that the intellectual exercise isn't fun, but in terms of a <em>story</em>, sometimes it's lacking.)<br /><br />So I can't give the book four stars, much as I enjoyed it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6492199093644409177">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/112169780?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 18, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3570506-melinda-snodgrass" class="user" title="Melinda Snodgrass">Melinda Snodgrass</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/47990.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552154161" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer112169780">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10505453193711826362">I came to Terry Pratchett late which meant I have this lovely, long line of books to graze through. Pratchett's Discworld is just our world, but amplified by the presence of witches and wizards, and Death is a character, and a lovely old duffer with a charming, if prickly, granddaughter. Rounding out the cast is the enigmatic ruler of THe Discworld's major city, and the various citizens of that city from thieves and beggars, and bankers, and trying to help keep order over this motley crew is the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10505453193711826362">I came to Terry Pratchett late which meant I have this lovely, long line of books to graze through. Pratchett's Discworld is just our world, but amplified by the presence of witches and wizards, and Death is a character, and a lovely old duffer with a charming, if prickly, granddaughter. Rounding out the cast is the enigmatic ruler of THe Discworld's major city, and the various citizens of that city from thieves and beggars, and bankers, and trying to help keep order over this motley crew is the Watch (police) under the command of Sam Vimes. The Watch books are my favorites in the series. (I have a soft spot and great admiration for police officers. They do a hard job.) So, I was delighted to discover this was a Watch book.<br /><br />Jingo is a tale of how war fever sweeps through the city, and how suddenly the people who run the local curry shop are The Enemy, and how we sometimes fight over absurdities. It reads like a primer for the idiotic war in Iraq, and I wish someone had stuck it under Bush's nose. The book is LOL funny in places, and you find yourself nodding wisely at his incisive social commentary.<br /><br />At any rate, if you haven't Pratchett you are missing a delight. Go and check himout.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10505453193711826362">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_466448492">

  <a href="/user/show/163750-callista" class="left imgcol" title="Callista "><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1189653481p2/163750.jpg" alt="Callista " /></a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/47990.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552154161" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/163750-callista?shelf=fantasy-satire" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-satire</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/163750-callista?shelf=favourite-characters" class="actionLinkLite">favourite-characters</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer466448492'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer466448492">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14424573056519326645">Samuel Vimes has become another of my favourite Discworld characters. In this story, he’s especially amazing, cleverly interpreting the law and putting himself in danger to help stop a war. He’s a wonderful combination of honesty, decency, persistence and cynicism. <br />As with the previous Watch novels, I enjoyed Carrot Ironfoundersson’s exploits here. He’s a delightful character, with his naïve honesty and natural goodness. He can get almost any situation under control. (Sometimes he makes me think</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14424573056519326645">Samuel Vimes has become another of my favourite Discworld characters. In this story, he’s especially amazing, cleverly interpreting the law and putting himself in danger to help stop a war. He’s a wonderful combination of honesty, decency, persistence and cynicism. <br />As with the previous Watch novels, I enjoyed Carrot Ironfoundersson’s exploits here. He’s a delightful character, with his naïve honesty and natural goodness. He can get almost any situation under control. (Sometimes he makes me think of Constable Benton Fraser of the RCMP from the TV show <em>Due South</em>, as Fraser had a similarly winning way with people, although Fraser was better educated. If only Carrot could master the finer points of punctuation and spelling.) And it’s fun to picture such a big, redheaded Scot type in some of the outrageous situations he finds himself in—including the sort of Lawrence of Arabia with football bits in this story. <br />I’m increasingly impressed with Lord Vetinari, the ruler of Ankh-Morpork, a consummate politician. The man is always a dozen steps ahead of everyone else and operates with such flair. I especially like his deadpan denials of skills he possesses or tricks he’s pulled. He could probably wipe the floor with most of the other fictional schemers ever invented. His efforts to defuse the war in this story are brilliant.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14424573056519326645">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer67163264">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14666997628238269377">Ankh-Morpork gets involved in a war with Klatch and the Discworld equivalent of Atlantis, Leshp, is about to rise. It is up to the Watch, Vimes, Colon, Carrot and all the rest to find a way to prevent it. In Jingo the Discworld looks into the dark heart of war, land disputes, assassination, science and weapons development, and prejudice. Of course, Terry Pratchett does it in his special way, sneaking it in here and there and occasionally hitting you head on with it. Of course, there is a lot mor</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14666997628238269377">Ankh-Morpork gets involved in a war with Klatch and the Discworld equivalent of Atlantis, Leshp, is about to rise. It is up to the Watch, Vimes, Colon, Carrot and all the rest to find a way to prevent it. In Jingo the Discworld looks into the dark heart of war, land disputes, assassination, science and weapons development, and prejudice. Of course, Terry Pratchett does it in his special way, sneaking it in here and there and occasionally hitting you head on with it. Of course, there is a lot more going on than just two nations setting out to wage war. Commander Vimes feels like he's out of touch and losing control of the Watch to Captain Carrot, who has been actively recruiting new guardsmen. Since Carrot joined the Watch, the number of guards has grown from three men to over forty men, or actually, creatures. Thanks to Carrot, the Watch now includes dwarfs, gargoyles, a golem, a troll, a gnome, a werewolf and a zombie. Carrot is, of course, totally oblivious to Vimes' concerns, as his only objective is to uphold the law and protect the citizens of Ankh-Morpork. He has the kind of charisma that lets him organize a football game between two armies poised at the brink of war, and make criminals beg to confess.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14666997628238269377">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer944446601886465858">I've read this a few times, actually. Some of the fine details of the plot I still didn't remember until I read it just now. Part of this might just be poor memory (it's sort of <em>nice</em> to have that for books, actually, as some of the contents still come as a surprise upon rereading. I may remember the gist, but how exactly it comes about is still "novel"), and part of it might be that some of the major plot points are actually a bit unclear, even after they are revealed, in a single conversation.<br /><br />I</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText944446601886465858">I've read this a few times, actually. Some of the fine details of the plot I still didn't remember until I read it just now. Part of this might just be poor memory (it's sort of <em>nice</em> to have that for books, actually, as some of the contents still come as a surprise upon rereading. I may remember the gist, but how exactly it comes about is still "novel"), and part of it might be that some of the major plot points are actually a bit unclear, even after they are revealed, in a single conversation.<br /><br />I like Pratchett tremendously, and of course it's worth a read, especially if you like following the Night Watch crew, but I wouldn't recommend it as a way of jumping into the series; there is much that you wouldn't really appreciate if you didn't already have some familiarity with the characters.<br /><br />As usual Pratchett is an astute observer of society and human nature; I like the notion that big crimes (wars) ought to be treated like small crimes (murders).</span>

              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1390900394">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14524918191783029761">Veni Vici... Vetinari!<br /><br />This is, without a shadow of a doubt, my favourite Watch book.. always has been, always will be. It's very quoteable too.. you have the bit where Vimes is thinking how much "Veni Vidi Vici" sounds like someone put some serious thought into it while sitting over a dictionary and going through all the V words to Reg's declaration that you would think a nail would be enough to keep his ear firmly attached to his head. <br /><br />I think I like it so much because it's a story with legs ..</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14524918191783029761">Veni Vici... Vetinari!<br /><br />This is, without a shadow of a doubt, my favourite Watch book.. always has been, always will be. It's very quoteable too.. you have the bit where Vimes is thinking how much "Veni Vidi Vici" sounds like someone put some serious thought into it while sitting over a dictionary and going through all the V words to Reg's declaration that you would think a nail would be enough to keep his ear firmly attached to his head. <br /><br />I think I like it so much because it's a story with legs .. it's not your typical murder story.. yes, there's a murder but Vimes and the crew get to stretch their geographical legs and go wander the Katchian deserts, they leave the confines of Ankh-Morpork. Yes, there are other stories where Vimes leaves AM but he's off on official business and then there's a murder or some crime that he needs to sort out .. this time, there's a death/attempted murder and he has to leave, following the ancient tradition of a Knight forming his own army, to sort things out. It's different. There's also quite a good side story with Vetinari, Colon and Nobby and their travels. It's different, it's funny, it's quoteable and our man Vimes gets a title upgrade for his troubles.<br /><br />Love it.<br /><br />Now we have another break to read the latest Mistborn book *hops about excitedly*<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14524918191783029761">...more</a>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1450175834">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2477288007470990701">What surprised me about this book was that apparently the story's inspiration was an actual historical event, <a rel="nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graham_Island_%28Sicily%29">the rising of Graham Island,</a> which just goes to show that truth can be every bit as strange as fiction.<br /><br />Anyways, I've been thinking lately about how crazy dangerous runaway nationalism is, so this book definitely hit home. It's a cautionary tale about how easily people will get worked up against 'those ____ people', wrapped in Terry Pratchett's usual hilarious satire.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/400356.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780061059063" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4091267-rebecca-huston?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4091267-rebecca-huston?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4091267-rebecca-huston?shelf=series" class="actionLinkLite">series</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4091267-rebecca-huston?shelf=discworld" class="actionLinkLite">discworld</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4091267-rebecca-huston?shelf=2013-mt-tbr-challenge" class="actionLinkLite">2013-mt-tbr-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer466461135">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer920803181119598084">This time, Terry Pratchett takes on the tricky aspect of war. But there's a heavy dash of humour, and cynicism and a harsh look at our own world. Leonard of Quirm plays a vital role, Sam Vines is up to his neck in all sorts of trouble, and we get to see a very rare side of the Patrician. Not to mention 71-Hour Ahmed. Some serious moments, and it's a very amusing, if dark, read. Five stars, and recommended. <br /><br />For the longer review, please go here:<br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.epinions.com/review/Jingo_by_Terry_Pratchett/content_630717910660" target="_blank">http://www.epinions.com/review/Jingo_...</a></span>

          </span>
        </div>

            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/159421-shan?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1378937496">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15636423725831777642">Well, it's Terry Pratchett, it's about the Watch, and it's got some really great pearls of wisdom (like the one about a crowd's intelligence being the square root of the number of people in it; and the one about Sgt Colon who'd had a broad education at the School of My Dad Always Said and the College of It Stands to Reason and was now a post-graduate student at the University of What Some Bloke in the Pub Told Me). I really, really like Terry Pratchett, especially when I read him after reading a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15636423725831777642">Well, it's Terry Pratchett, it's about the Watch, and it's got some really great pearls of wisdom (like the one about a crowd's intelligence being the square root of the number of people in it; and the one about Sgt Colon who'd had a broad education at the School of My Dad Always Said and the College of It Stands to Reason and was now a post-graduate student at the University of What Some Bloke in the Pub Told Me). I really, really like Terry Pratchett, especially when I read him after reading anything dark or discouraging or depressing. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[ I love the idea of stopping wars by arresting the troublemakers who are trying to start them, and getting the people on both sides to shake hands and play soccer instead. Wish we had Vimes and Captain Carrot around to end our seemingly endless wars. There's some interesting stuff in here like the plot to kill the prince, reminiscent of the assassination that started World War I; and of course all the stuff that is so painfully parallel to the Iraq/Afghanistan/etc. wars, at least before the most recent horrors. But it's so cheerful in Pratchett, and there are wise people on both sides who can see the ridiculousness and make it stop. Where's 71-hour Ahmed when you need him?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17359098158866221012">“Despojos de la calle, había dicho una vez Colon. <br />Despojos de la puta calle, porque la gente normal no se metía a madero”<br /><br />Una isla emerge en medio del mar, a medio camino de Ankh-Morpork y Klatch e inmediatamente surgen las primeras disputas sobre su soberanía y derechos, lo cual reabre las rencillas entre estos dos países, que hace demasiado que no van a la guerra el uno contra el otro. <div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1227138095">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1227138095" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1902039-ali" class="left imgcol" title="Ali"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1434174511p2/1902039.jpg" alt="Ali" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1227138095?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 02, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1902039-ali" class="user" title="Ali">Ali</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/47990.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552154161" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1902039-ali?shelf=read-in-2015" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1227138095">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5053220724716903109">Disappointing. <br /><br />As in Men at Arms, Pratchett takes on racism and xenophobia - and doesn't do a good job. It's heavy on "isn't it funny how different cultures all think foreigners are weird", without any real attempt to deal with power (ie even acknowledging that white colonial racism is not equivalent to other peoples' prejudices). The use of actual real-world racial slurs was also deeply unpleasant, and not used to good effect. The book also still relied on a lot of stereotypes of Muslims and/or</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5053220724716903109">Disappointing. <br /><br />As in Men at Arms, Pratchett takes on racism and xenophobia - and doesn't do a good job. It's heavy on "isn't it funny how different cultures all think foreigners are weird", without any real attempt to deal with power (ie even acknowledging that white colonial racism is not equivalent to other peoples' prejudices). The use of actual real-world racial slurs was also deeply unpleasant, and not used to good effect. The book also still relied on a lot of stereotypes of Muslims and/or Arab people. Sometimes this felt self-aware and deliberate (although ineffective as a way of actually making a point about racism), but it also ran a lot deeper than that in terms of how the Klatchians were actually represented in the book. And as I've been noticing more and more as I reread the series, women are treated as amusing curiosities rather than, you know, people.<br /><br />The plot also seemed convoluted and rambly, but to be honest I wasn't paying that much attention (for the reasons above).<br /><br />If that was an unknown writer rather than Pratchett, I wouldn't have stuck with it until the end. Some great quotes as always, and some funny moments, but all in all really disappointing.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5053220724716903109">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4502046936915167426">my book - and i had the right ISBN - only had 323 pages. i was not a fan of this book from the start based on premise alone. i understand the political satire very well but at the first mention of 'towel heads' i seized up and wanted no further part in it. i had to wait for the tiffany aching books to come in so i kept reading anyway, as it isn't like me to not finish a book even if i dislike it. i mean, i read the stand, the extended newer version, which was awful, simply because i started it.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4502046936915167426">my book - and i had the right ISBN - only had 323 pages. i was not a fan of this book from the start based on premise alone. i understand the political satire very well but at the first mention of 'towel heads' i seized up and wanted no further part in it. i had to wait for the tiffany aching books to come in so i kept reading anyway, as it isn't like me to not finish a book even if i dislike it. i mean, i read the stand, the extended newer version, which was awful, simply because i started it. <br /><br />there were some new characters i was introduced to in discworld that i liked, especially leonard of quirm, angua, vetinari, and nobby. but considering politics as they are, and living where i live now, it wasn't something i needed nor wanted to read. it wasn't fun, entertaining nor enlightening in any way. in a way i felt almost like my subset was left out too - the huge masses who loudly voice their opinions against the conflicts or rationally can see what violence over petty bullshit costs people in the long run even monetarily, and not equating 'different' as 'lesser-than'. <br /><br />considering these concepts are something i still have to friggin' argue with people about overall i just found the book frustrating really. i'm sure many people enjoyed it and took a lot out of it but not me. mass ignorance is a battle i can't win and i feel like all the book did was act as a reminder of how many different times this crap has happened and how no matter what i say or do to try to convince anyone otherwise, or instill rational thought, it's futile. </span>

      <a href="/user/show/17772356-erin-fitzgerald" class="user" title="Erin Fitzgerald">Erin Fitzgerald</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer691154695">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16459319099163103223">The entire world needs to read this book right now. The relevance is uncanny. As always, Terry Pratchett (may God rest his soul in the best bunk of the starry sky) blew me away without even realizing my feet were being swept out from under me until it was too late, and I was in so deep I didn't even want to try getting out.<br /><br />Life lessons taught in this book could do everyone a world of good. You always learn more about yourself, sometimes than you ever wanted to know, from his books. And this is m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16459319099163103223">The entire world needs to read this book right now. The relevance is uncanny. As always, Terry Pratchett (may God rest his soul in the best bunk of the starry sky) blew me away without even realizing my feet were being swept out from under me until it was too late, and I was in so deep I didn't even want to try getting out.<br /><br />Life lessons taught in this book could do everyone a world of good. You always learn more about yourself, sometimes than you ever wanted to know, from his books. And this is more than true in a book about just how utterly ludicrous the conflicts people can get into truly are. Because, whether or not we want to see it, from the littlest to the biggest fight, we have in this book what it all really boils down to. <br /><br />The strength and realness of the characters are the backbone of any of his works. The fact that we can recognize ourselves in them, or around them, is exactly what makes us see things so clearly. The revelations come fast and hard. Us and all we hold dear are laid bare before our own eyes, often with excruciating, pinpoint humor.<br /><br />I am a better person each time I read one of his books.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16459319099163103223">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_112274299">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/112274299" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3948872-laura" class="left imgcol" title="Laura"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1301720220p2/3948872.jpg" alt="Laura" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/112274299?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 25, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3948872-laura" class="user" title="Laura">Laura</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/47990.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552154161" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Laura by: </span>
      Diana Walker
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=cthulhu" class="actionLinkLite">cthulhu</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=politics" class="actionLinkLite">politics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=fanfiction" class="actionLinkLite">fanfiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=myth" class="actionLinkLite">myth</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=dogs" class="actionLinkLite">dogs</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=government" class="actionLinkLite">government</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=metatexts" class="actionLinkLite">metatexts</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=satire" class="actionLinkLite">satire</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=war" class="actionLinkLite">war</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=atlantis" class="actionLinkLite">atlantis</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=nyarlothotep" class="actionLinkLite">nyarlothotep</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3948872-laura?shelf=apocalypse" class="actionLinkLite">apocalypse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer112274299">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4901661773112677396">Something wreathed with tentacles rises from the seabed between Klatch and Ankh Morpork, both of which apparently think they need a little more Lovecraftian landscapes in their lives. Someone tries to assassinate a prince in a convoy. War is declared. Meanwhile, Leonardo of Quirm, Ankh-Morpork’s own Menlo Park\Manhattan Project\Little town of Eureka figures out how to build submarines and that if you squish certain types of matter really hard, they go boom. Vetinari takes the submarine and a tub</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4901661773112677396">Something wreathed with tentacles rises from the seabed between Klatch and Ankh Morpork, both of which apparently think they need a little more Lovecraftian landscapes in their lives. Someone tries to assassinate a prince in a convoy. War is declared. Meanwhile, Leonardo of Quirm, Ankh-Morpork’s own Menlo Park\Manhattan Project\Little town of Eureka figures out how to build submarines and that if you squish certain types of matter really hard, they go boom. Vetinari takes the submarine and a tube to Klatch. Vimes decides to chase a suspect rather than defend his city. Wackiness ensues. <br /><br />	This book plays with some big themes. Things that we can’t quite see that will destroy us; the Lovecraftian terrors lurking in the waters; the Lovecraftian terrors lurking in politics and little tubes; the relation between a people and their rightful leaders; the relation between kings and racism. <br /><br />	Does it deliver? Maybe. When I was in law school, I almost stopped reading for pleasure. Almost. As the Terry Pratchett books were published, I read them hungrily. Voraciously. I remember the thrill seeing how this book played with things we were playing with in class. Things that hurt and delighted. Pratchett’s books have always made me happy and made me feel a little less alienated. <br /><br />	Reading it nearly 20 years later, it doesn’t seem as deep; the play doesn’t keep unfolding the way some of the books still do for me. The humanist propaganda – which I love – is not as graceful as I remember. <br /><br />	But still, I love it. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4901661773112677396">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_953574989">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/953574989" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9098678-scott-holstad" class="left imgcol" title="Scott Holstad"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1335536381p2/9098678.jpg" alt="Scott Holstad" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/953574989?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 17, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9098678-scott-holstad" class="user" title="Scott Holstad">Scott Holstad</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/47990.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552154161" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer953574989">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10250807251806028549">I can't quite put my finger on it, but I wasn't as taken with this Discworld novel as I usually am. The Discworld equivalent of Atlantis, Leshp, rises mysteriously in the sea between Ankh-Morpork and Klatch, causing both sides to claim it and ultimately go to war over it. It's up to the Night Watch, led by Vimes, as well as the Patrician, Lord Venitary, and a couple of the Watchmen to prevent it. I guess the trouble is, while still filled with Pratchett's humor and satire, it really dragged for</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10250807251806028549">I can't quite put my finger on it, but I wasn't as taken with this Discworld novel as I usually am. The Discworld equivalent of Atlantis, Leshp, rises mysteriously in the sea between Ankh-Morpork and Klatch, causing both sides to claim it and ultimately go to war over it. It's up to the Night Watch, led by Vimes, as well as the Patrician, Lord Venitary, and a couple of the Watchmen to prevent it. I guess the trouble is, while still filled with Pratchett's humor and satire, it really dragged for me, through the middle right up to the end of the book. I can't think of another Pratchett book I've read that I can say that about. I kept wondering when I would get to the end. I really wasn't that interested in what happened. Of course, Pratchett deals with big topics like war, racism, science and weapons development, etc., and that's somewhat interesting -- in his approach to these topics. But still, I was just bored. I'm sure this is a one off, as I'm already in another Discworld book and it seems typically great. I guess I'd recommend this for a Discworld fan, but if you're new to the series, I wouldn't start with this one.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10250807251806028549">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_303979277">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/303979277" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7915393-simon-mcleish" class="left imgcol" title="Simon Mcleish"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1347099463p2/7915393.jpg" alt="Simon Mcleish" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/303979277?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7915393-simon-mcleish" class="user" title="Simon Mcleish">Simon Mcleish</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/47990.Jingo" title="Paperback 9780552154161" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7915393-simon-mcleish?shelf=owned" class="actionLinkLite">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer303979277">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13695875805769611533">Originally published on my blog <a rel="nofollow" href="http://simonsbookblog.blogspot.co.uk/1999/01/terry-pratchett-jingo-1997.html">here</a> in January 1999.<br /><br />The twenty-first Discworld novel tackles a most depressing subject - the warmongering, violent side of human nature. The scenario is that the lost continent of Leshp has risen again from the seabed, to be claimed by both Ankh-Morpork and the empire of Klatch. War seems to be inevitable, as patriotism apparently spontaneously breaks out in Ankh-Morpork. The City Watch are once again the centre of things as Commander Vimes tries to work out who</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13695875805769611533">Originally published on my blog <a rel="nofollow" href="http://simonsbookblog.blogspot.co.uk/1999/01/terry-pratchett-jingo-1997.html">here</a> in January 1999.<br /><br />The twenty-first Discworld novel tackles a most depressing subject - the warmongering, violent side of human nature. The scenario is that the lost continent of Leshp has risen again from the seabed, to be claimed by both Ankh-Morpork and the empire of Klatch. War seems to be inevitable, as patriotism apparently spontaneously breaks out in Ankh-Morpork. The City Watch are once again the centre of things as Commander Vimes tries to work out who is guilty of the crime of attempted murder through warmongering.<br /><br />The book itself is fairly sombre in tone compared to the rest of the series, despite the convoluted farcical plot. War is a serious subject and the inhumanity of war is not something to be taken lightly (though it has been used to produce some of the greatest humour of the century, such as Heller's Catch 22). There are many obvious parallels intended between the patriots in Ankh-Morpork and the British and French generals of the First World War. Even eighty years after the end of that war, the stupidities of these generals is more material for tragedy than comedy, and even the best comedies which deal with it (Oh What a Lovely War, or Blackadder Goes Forth for example) have strong tragic elements which are almost entirely missing from this novel. On the other hand, the tragedy is so strong that even the more tragic works dealing with the War use comic effects (examples of this include Remarque's All Quiet on the Western Front and many of the poems of Wilfred Owen and Siegfried Sassoon.)<br /><br />The way that Pratchett writes about a non-naturalistic world in which he makes parallels with real history dilutes the tragedy while at the same time subduing the comedy. The fact that Jingo is the twenty-first Discworld novel tempts the reviewer to say that the series is coming of age, but that is not really the case; no serious points are made which have not been better made elsewhere, and Pratchett writes better when he does not even try to make serious points.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13695875805769611533">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett">About Terry Pratchett</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett"><img alt="1654" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1235562205p2/1654.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer13772045367533720200">Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <em>The Carpet People</em>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br /><br />Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, including his first Discworld novel,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13772045367533720200">Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <em>The Carpet People</em>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br /><br />Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, including his first Discworld novel, <em>The Color of Magic</em>, in 1983. In 1987, he turned to writing full time. <br /><br />There are over 40 books in the Discworld series, of which four are written for children. The first of these, <em>The Amazing Maurice and His Educated Rodents</em>, won the Carnegie Medal. <br /><br />A non-Discworld book, <em>Good Omens</em>, his 1990 collaboration with Neil Gaiman, has been a longtime bestseller and was reissued in hardcover by William Morrow in early 2006 (it is also available as a mass market paperback - Harper Torch, 2006 - and trade paperback - Harper Paperbacks, 2006). <br /><br />In 2008, Harper Children's published Terry's standalone non-Discworld YA novel, <em>Nation</em>. Terry published <em>Snuff</em> in October 2011. <br /><br />Regarded as one of the most significant contemporary English-language satirists, Pratchett has won numerous literary awards, was named an Officer of the British Empire “for services to literature” in 1998, and has received honorary doctorates from University of Warwick in 1999, the University of Portsmouth in 2001, the University of Bath in 2003, the University of Bristol in 2004, Buckinghamshire New University in 2008, the University of Dublin in 2008, Bradford University in 2009, University of Winchester in 2009, and The Open University in 2013 for his contribution to Public Service.<br /><br />In Dec. of 2007, Pratchett disclosed that he had been diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease. On 18 Feb, 2009, he was knighted by Queen Elizabeth II.<br /><br />He was awarded the World Fantasy Life Achievement Award in 2010.<br /><br />Sir Terry Pratchett passed away on 12th March 2015.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13772045367533720200">...more</a>

    </div>
    <a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett" class="actionLink moreLink">More about Terry Pratchett...</a>
